    Thieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxamides as New Reversible Inhibitors of Histone Lysine Demethylase KDM1A/LSD1. Part 2: Structure-Based Drug Design and Structure–Activity Relationship

    Abstract

    The balance of methylation levels at histone H3 lysine 4 (H3K4) is regulated by KDM1A (LSD1). KDM1A is overexpressed in several tumor types, thus representing an emerging target for the development of novel cancer therapeutics. We have previously described (Part 1, DOI 10.1021.acs.jmedchem.6b01018) the identification of thieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxamides as novel reversible inhibitors of KDM1A, whose preliminary exploration resulted in compound 2 with biochemical IC50 = 160 nM. We now report the structure-guided optimization of this chemical series based on multiple ligand/KDM1A-CoRest cocrystal structures, which led to several extremely potent inhibitors. In particular, compounds 46, 49, and 50 showed single-digit nanomolar IC50 values for in vitro inhibition of KDM1A, with high selectivity in secondary assays. In THP-1 cells, these compounds transcriptionally affected the expression of genes regulated by KDM1A such as CD14, CD11b, and CD86. Moreover, 49 and 50 showed a remarkable anticlonogenic cell growth effect on MLL-AF9 human leukemia cells.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Aberrant post-translational modifications of chromatin have been associated with the onset and progression of several human diseases, including cancer. In particular, the N-methylation status of histones is one of the crucial factors that regulate transcription and eventually control enhancement or repression of gene expression. Histones can be methylated on lysine and arginine residues by histone methyl transferase proteins. Lysines demethylation is specifically catalyzed by two families of enzymes: (i) lysine specific demethylases (KDM1). which are flavine adenine dinucleotide (FAD) dependent proteins related to monoamine oxidases (MAOs) and catalyze methyl removal from mono- and dimethylated histones H3K9 and H3K4, and (ii) JmjC-domain containing histone demethylases (KDM2, KDM3, KDM4, KDM5, JARID2, KDM6, KDM7, KDM8), which utilize α-ketoglutarate (2OG) and iron (Fe(II)) as a cofactor, to catalyze demethylation of mono-, di-, and trimethylated lysines at multiple sites.(1) Two human FAD-dependent demethylases have been identified: LSD1, also known as KDM1A,(2) and LSD2, also known as KDM1B.(3) KDM1A is highly expressed in different cancer types, including acute myeloid leukemia,(4) prostate cancer,(5, 6) ER-negative breast cancer,(7) and neuroblastoma(8) and often its overexpression correlates with poor prognosis.
    After the discovery of tranylcypromine as a weak irreversible KDM1A inhibitor,(9) an extensive effort has been dedicated to the field.(10-18) Three irreversible tranylcypromine derivatives from GSK, Oryzon (see Figure 1: GSK2879552,(19, 20) ORY1001,(21, 22) depicted as 55) and Incyte (INC059872(23) undisclosed structure) are now in clinical stages. Conversely, reports on the preclinical development of low molecular weight reversible inhibitors have been much more limited.(24-26) In particular, SP-2509 was reported by Salarius to be active in xenograft models,(27, 28) although the compound itself did not demonstrate a robust correlation between biochemical activity against KDM1A and inhibition of cell proliferation.(24)
    As reported in Part 1, (DOI 10.1021.acs.jmedchem.6b01018),(29) from an high throughput screening campaign (HTS), we identified a promising chemical series, namely 4H-thieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxamide(30) (represented by compound 1, Figure 2) with inhibitory activity against KDM1A. Initial SAR studies indicated that substitutions at the phenyl ring of 1 could increase the inhibitory potency, resulting in compound 2, with an IC50 of 162 nM for KDM1A inhibition.
    Docking experiments of compound 2 allowed dual pose orientations (see Figure 3) directing the positively charged piperidine tail toward two negatively charged regions. We synthesized a series of derivatives able to reach different portions of the protein to solve the ambiguity of docking results and to increase inhibitory potency. The new compounds prepared gave us a clear indication of the relevant substituents for inhibitory activity and their interactions with protein active site of this novel class of noncovalent KDM1A inhibitors.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Chemistry

    A general synthesis for the derivatives reported is depicted in Scheme 1 (method A). Ethyl 4H-thieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxylate (3a)(31) was methylated(32) at the pyrrole nitrogen and hydrolyzed to the acid intermediate 3c. Reaction of the corresponding acyl chloride with the appropriate aniline (3e–28e) gave final amides 3-21 and 22f–28f, in which the piperidine nitrogen is BOC-protected. Finally, compounds 22f–28f were deprotected to 22–28 by acidic treatment and compound 22 was treated with the appropriate aldehydes under reductive amination conditions to give 29 or 30 or with acetyl chloride to give 31 (Scheme 1).
    The general synthesis of the anilino derivatives is represented in Scheme 2: (bromomethyl)-nitro-benzenes (15g–17g) were coupled with the appropriate biphenols to give 15i–19i. Mitsunobu reaction of tert-butyl 4-hydroxypiperidine-1-carboxylate with the desired nitrophenols allowed the preparation of derivatives 16j–18j, which were transformed into the N-methyl derivatives 16d–18d, by Boc removal after acidic treatment, and reductive amination with formaldehyde and NaBH3CN (15d and 19d were instead prepared by reaction of 15i and 19i with 1-methylpiperidin-4-ol). Nitro reduction originated desired anilines 15e–19e and 22e.
    Different methods used to prepare anilino derivatives (20e, 21e, 23e–28e) are reported in Supporting Information.
    Alternatively, a general procedure for the synthesis of the compounds is depicted in Scheme 3. The basic moiety BOC protected was inserted at the last step followed by usual protective group removal. The nitro group in compound 15i was reduced via catalytic hydrogenation to give the aniline 33k, which was condensed to compound 3c previously transformed into acyl chloride. The thus obtained 33l through Mitsunobu reaction with alcohols R5-OH generated 33f–43f that were further deprotected to 33–43 (Table 4).
    Ortho,meta-disubstituted compounds (Tables 5–7) were prepared according to Schemes 4 or 5.
    In Scheme 4, 2-(hydroxymethyl)-6-nitro-benzoate 45n, obtained from 7-nitro-3H-isobenzofuran-1-one(33) by heating in 1 M sodium hydroxide and THF, was treated with NaH and an excess of the required alkyl halides, generating the 45v and 50v. Subsequent reduction of the ester gave the alcohols 45w and 50w, which were converted into their bromo derivatives 45g and 50g. Reaction with hydroquinone furnished the phenols 45l and 50l. Phenolic function was thus accessible to Mitsunobu reaction to give 45d–50d, their nitro groups were transformed into anilines 45e–50e, useful building blocks for the benzamide preparation 45f–50f, obtained by reacting with 3c or 49c. Boc-protecting groups were finally removed to give the compounds 45–50.
    Alternatively, disubstituted derivatives were prepared according to Scheme 5: 45n was alkylated with isopropyl iodide, providing 52o, which was further converted into 52p by protecting the alcoholic group with TBDSCl. The carboxylic function was reduced to 52q and the thus formed alcohol transformed into its mesylate 52r to facilitate the reaction with hydroquinone and the ether formation in 52l. Free phenolic oxygen reacted with tert-butyl 4-hydroxypiperidine-1-carboxylate to give 52d, which, after reduction to the anilino derivative 52e, reacted with the carboxylic acid 3c to give intermediate 52s. Removal of the protective group (52t), transformation of the alcoholic function into a mesylate group (52u) and subsequent reaction with morpholine or an appropriate alcohol gave the BOC intermediates 52f–54f, which were finally hydrolyzed to give 52–54.

    SAR Investigation

    The evidence that docking runs allowed dual placement of piperidine moiety and the lack of unambiguous data from X-ray crystallography for compound 2 prompted us to perform an electrostatic potential analysis of the protein active site (Figure 3), identifying two regions of potential interactions for positively charged piperidine tail, both justifying such an increase in potency of compound 2 with respect to 1. One region is located around Asp375, above the thienopyrrole plane. The other is located around Asp555 and Asp556, which are located below the thienopyrrole plane. The compound (green in Figure 3) would adopt an extended conformation to interact with Asp375, whereas the interaction with Asp556 would force the inhibitor in a U-shaped conformation (pink in Figure 3). The two poses of 2, obtained with different docking programs and submitted to molecular dynamics, showed complexes stability sufficient to be both considered as starting points for SAR exploration (see Supporting Information for details).
    Taking into consideration the results obtained when meta position of the hit was modified, as described in Part 1,(29) we planned a careful SAR exploration, initially directed to the ortho, meta, and para positions on the benzamide ring and subsequently to the optimal residue identified. Different spacers, hydroquinone vs resorcinol or cathecol, and different terminal basic moieties, were also investigated and to conclude the best results were combined to generate ortho–meta disubstituted derivatives (see Figure 4).

    Ortho, meta, and para Benzamide Investigation

    The investigation on the benzamide was designed with the following aims:
    1.

    to explore the area occupied by the substituents on benzamide function,

    2.

    to modulate the substituents’ contribution to the inhibitory activity,

    3.

    to verify whether the structural modifications of the ligand could provide X-ray structures.

    As reported in Table 1, the substituents were selected from those already tested in the meta series.(29) The expansion was fully explored to gain a complete SAR and to direct the substituents toward the two negatively charged regions. We used piperidine as a basic moiety to take advantage of activity increase shown in the meta series reported in Part 1.(29) Whereas methoxy, methoxymethyl, aryloxy methyl, and arylmethoxy substituents in the benzamide moiety maintained high micromolar activity when applied to the meta position (1, 7, 10, 13), they brought instead to almost inactive compounds when placed in ortho and para position (6, 8, 9, 11, 12, 14). When the chain bearing a piperidinoxyphenoxymethyl group was moved from meta to ortho and para, the potency of the unsubstituted term 3 was however improved, although while para derivative 17 resulted in the micromolar range, ortho derivatives 15 and 22 resulted in being slightly more active than the corresponding meta ones, 16 and 2. We report that several attempts to place unsubstituted piperidine in position para failed for synthetic hurdles during BOC deprotection of the intermediate.
    Table 1. Ortho, meta, and para Investigation on the Benzamide:KDM1A Inhibitory Activity (IC50 μMa)
    Table a

    All the compounds reported in table have been prepared according to Scheme 1. Data are expressed as the mean of at least two determinations ± standard deviation.

    This preliminary exploration resulted in being quite satisfactory. One of the most potent ortho derivatives 15 easily crystallized, and the analysis of X-ray structure provided an explanation for potency improvement (see Figure 5A). Ortho derivative 15 accommodates the benzamide moiety, favoring aromatic–aromatic interactions, and directs the basic terminal residue toward one of the two negatively charged regions (Asp555). Compound 15 unambiguously follows one of the two hypothesized positions, pointing downward with respect to the thienopyrrole plane. The ligand occupies the catalytic site of KDM1A, and the size and the shape of the active site renders the packed “U shaped” conformation as the most preferred one. Main hydrophobic interactions have been evidenced with no visible polar contacts. The thienopyrrole moiety is comprised between residues Val333, Thr335, Leu663, Tyr765, and Thr814 into a cage that is delimitated by flavin. The second ring system is packed between residues Val333, Phe543, and Trp699.
    Compound 17 (see Figure 5B), a para substituted derivative that showed to inhibit KDM1A, albeit with considerably less potency (2.5 μM), has also been crystallized. The benzamide moiety of 17 is forced out of the plane, altering the geometry of its aromatic–aromatic interaction with flavin, a possible explanation for the lower potency, while the terminal basic moiety located in proximity to Glu379 (2.9 A) could contribute to the residual activity.
    To conclude activity and structural comparisons between ortho, meta, and para derivatives, we speculate that meta derivative 2 could adopt a conformation similar to 15 but having interaction with Asp556 instead of Asp555. The para derivative 17 interacts in the upward negative region (Glu379), reachable also by obtained docking pose of 2, but the 5–5 system planarity distortion and the lesser biochemical potency suggest that this conformation is unlikely the one adopted by 2. The possible explanation for the inactivity of shorter derivatives (4–6, 8–14) is that only meta derivatives can reach interactions with Gln358 (docking results, see Supporting Information).
    Finally, compound 15 was also evaluated to define its biochemical mechanism of inhibition. According to Figure 6A, Lineweaver–Burk plot indicated a nearly competitive type inhibition for compound 15, with a Ki = 2.3 μM. Accordingly, in Figure 6B, we report the overlap of X-ray structures of 15 with 2V1D,(34) clearly showing that the ortho substituent on the benzamide moiety occupies the same region of Met-Thr-Arg-Ala residues of the substrate.

    Ortho, meta, and para Substitution of the Hydroquinone

    The influence of the ortho, meta, and para position of the biphenol moiety was investigated (see Table 2), the meta being detrimental (18 resulted in being 3-fold less active than 15) while the ortho being barely active (19 IC50= 68.2 μM). This trend in potency is justified by observing that the exit vector orientation for substituents on para biphenol moiety is directed toward Asp555.
    Table 2. Ortho, meta, and para Substitution of the Hydroquinoneb
    Table a

    Data are expressed as the mean of at least two determinations ± standard deviation.

    Table b

    All the compounds reported in Table have been prepared according to Scheme 1.

    We continued our investigation by further modifying the lateral chain as depicted in Figure 4: specifically, only ortho substituted derivatives were prepared, both for the higher potency of the analogues and for the advantage of having a support from X-ray crystallography.

    Modifications on the Spacer

    Several compounds were designed and prepared to alter the hydroquinonic feature of 22 (see Table 3). The phenyl ring has been replaced with piperidinomethyl moiety 44, resulting in a much less potent compound. Also amide in place of ether (25) or nitrogen in place of oxygen (24 and 26) caused a reduction of potency. The position of oxygen seemed irrelevant, 16 (Table 1) being equipotent to 20, its removal instead barely influenced the activity (23 vs 22) and was of a much greater impact when it occurred at the distal oxygen as in 26 or 21.
    Table 3. Modifications on the Spacerc
    Table c

    All the compounds reported in Table have been prepared according to Scheme 1.

    Table a

    Data are expressed as the mean of at least two determinations ± standard deviation.

    Table b

    Prepared according to Supporting Information.

    To explain these trends various aspects have been investigated:

    • Conformational analysis,

    • HOMO–LUMO energies and localization,

    • partial charges on NH (N-Me).

    While for 44 and for 25 the conformational accessible space can quite easily explain the activity decrease, when examining the couples 22/26 and 15/21, there are neither differences in conformational space nor in docking results or in nitrogen partial charges. Assuming that electronic configuration might influence the affinity of the ligand and considering the aromatic–aromatic interaction between FAD and 5–5 thienopyrrole system, we investigated HOMO–LUMO localization and energies (see Supporting Information for details). We have noticed that the decrease in activity is associated with the HOMO shifting toward piperidino ring, away from the 5–5 system. The hypothesis that this shift may decrease the strength of the electronic interaction between FAD and 5–5 system is under further investigations based on more accurate ab initio models.

    Different Basic Moieties (ortho)

    The inspection of the ring bearing the basic nitrogen was also extensively performed as reported in Table 4: when 4-piperidinol was converted to 3-piperidinol as in 41 or shortened to 3-pirrolidinole (42) and azetidin-3yl-methanol (27), or when the cycle was opened to N-methylaminepropanol (43) or guanidinomethyl (28), the activity significantly dropped from 2- to 10-fold. Equipotent derivatives were obtained with 4-aminocyclohexanol, where the cis isomer 36 resulted in being 2-fold more potent than the trans isomer (40) and (1S,5R)-8-azabicyclo[3.2.1]octan-3-ol (37). The activity significantly improved with 4-azepanole (35) and pyrrolidin-3-ylmethanol (34), in particular its 3R enantiomers (33) that showed an IC50 of 18 nM.
    Table 4. Different Basic Moieties
    Table a

    Data are expressed as the mean of at least two determinations ± standard deviation.

    Table b

    Prepared according to Scheme 1.

    Table c

    Prepared according to Scheme 3.

    A small set of residues was prepared to alter the basicity of the terminal nitrogen and the space occupancy of 22. Ethyl derivative 29 resulted in being slightly less active, while enlarging substituent as in 30 or removing the basicity (31), as expected, resulted in complete inactivity.
    34 was crystallized (see Figure 7) and showed the same orientation and interaction pattern with protein residues as the parent 15. The length of the chain and the protein residue flexibility allow interactions of nitrogen atoms with Asp555, where (3R)-pyrrolidine group in 33 shows the best activity among the series.

    Ortho–meta Disubstituted Derivatives

    By observing X-ray crystal structure of 1 and 34 superimposed (Figure 8), it was evident that ortho and meta substituents could occupy different pockets of the KDM1A active site, thus suggesting that their copresence could facilitate the interaction with the enzyme. With this in mind, we approached the ortho–meta double substitution, expanding separately the two positions according to Tables 5 and 6. We observed a further improvement in the activity: 46 in fact gave a single-digit nanomolar IC50.
    Table 5. Ortho–meta Disubstituted Derivatives: SAR on Piperidinol

    Combining Best Features

    The last three compounds reported in Table 7 were prepared by combining all those features that had demonstrated positive impact in terms of biochemical potency. 49 was obtained by applying the best substitution on pyrrolic nitrogen (ethyl group identified in Part 1) and compound 50 by adding an ethyloxymethyl in meta: their IC50s were more or less identical as well as their selectivity versus KDM1B and MAOs.
    Table 7. Combining Best Featuresc
    Table a

    Data are expressed as the mean of at least two determinations ± standard deviation.

    Table b

    Fold increase mRNA expression, reported as mean values of several replicates (≥2) at the fixed dose of 1 μM for 46, 49, and 50 and 0.5 μM for 55, measured after 24 h of compound treatment.

    Table c

    All the compounds reported in Table have been prepared according to Scheme 5.

    49 and 50 have been crystallized, confirming the working hypothesis (Figure 9). The increased potency of disubstituted compounds can be related to additional interactions between Gln358 and ethyloxymethyl chain, which is also responsible of conformational restraints on the longer chain, thus with entropic gain that further induces the U-shaped conformation.

    Biology

    The ability of the best compounds to inhibit KDM1A in human acute myeloid THP-1 cells was evaluated. THP-1 cells, characterized by the MLL-AF9 translocation, were selected for the strong experimental evidence, demonstrating the relevance of KDM1A in sustaining the oncogenic potential of MLL-AF9 leukemia stem cells.(35) Moreover, the absence of evident effect on global level of histone H3K4 methylation after genetic ablation of KDM1A both in cancer cells(36) and hematopoietc cells(37) let us follow KDM1A inhibition in cells, looking at the transcriptional effect on known KDM1A regulating genes (see Supporting Information, Figure S2). To this purpose, THP-1 cells were incubated with the selected inhibitors at the fixed concentration of 1 μM, and after 24 h, CD14, CD86, and CD11b mRNA levels were measured by quantitative RT-PCR. As reported in Table 7, all the tested compounds, similarly to 55, were able to induce an increase of mRNA expression of the selected genes. The observed effects being similar to what has been obtained by KDM1A down-regulation by shRNA demonstrated their ability to block KDM1A activity in cells inducing differentiation. To additionally confirm induction of differentiation in AML cells mediated by our compounds, we evaluated the expression of CD86 and CD11b by flow cytometry in THP-1 cells treated for 48 h. As reported in Supporting Information, Figure S2, all the three compounds induced a clear increase of the expression of the two differentiation markers with respect to vehicle treated cells. The antiproliferative activity of 49, 46, and 50 was determined on three different leukemia cell lines: THP-1, NB4, and MV4-11, representative of different subtypes of AMLs. THP-1 and MV4-11 are both characterized by MLL translocation and MV4-11 by a concurrent presence of FLT3 mutation. As shown in Table 8, the compounds tested are demonstrated to exert antiproliferative effects on the evaluated cell lines at a micromolar level.
    Table 8. Antiproliferation Data
    Table a

    Data are expressed as the mean of at least two determinations ± standard deviation.

    To verify the ability of 49 and 50 to induce apoptosis or to affect cell cycle progression, annexin V–propidium iodide (PI) double staining was used on THP-1 cells. Data (as reported in Supporting Information, Figure S3) showed that 72 h treatment at 1 μM of 49 and 50 and 0.5 μM of 55 did not induce cell death, in agreement with the known pro-differentiation effects of KDM1A inhibition in AML cells.,(34, 45) Finally, the anticlonogenic potential of 49 and 50 on human acute myeloid leukemia THP-1 was determined. As reported in the Table 9 both of the compounds strongly inhibited the colony growth of THP-1 cells and, similarly to 55, induced differentiation, as demonstrated by the increased expression of CD86 marker.
    Table 9. Anticlonogenic Potential
    compdTHP-1a,b methylcellulose colony formation % inhibitionCD86b,c

    c

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    We have reported the development of thieno[3,2-b]pyrrole KDM1A inhibitors, a novel chemical series identified by an HTS campaign, which potently and reversibly inhibit KDM1A. Several different synthetic pathways allowed an extensive exploration of various portions of the molecule and led to the identification of the main structural requirements for the inhibitory activity. This work has been supported by multiple ligand/KDM1A-CoRest cocrystal structures, which rationalized the gain in potency due to polar interaction with two negatively charged areas of the catalytic site. We identified critical molecular features for potency: ortho substitution in the benzamide portion brought to more stable complex with the enzyme, giving optimal inhibitory activity and allowing the solution of multiple crystal structures. 3D information thus obtained guided subsequent optimization. Para substitution of the hydroquinone moiety was mandatory for the potency. The lack of substituents on the terminal nitrogen facilitates the interaction with the enzyme and the pyrrolidin-3-yl-methanol residue 34, in particular, its 3R enantiomer 33, resulted as the most efficacious basic moiety among those explored. Concurrent bisubstitution of the benzamide at positions ortho and meta brought to a clear improvement of the potency, generating single-digit nanomolar inhibitors 46, 49, and 50. These compounds were characterized for their cellular activity: they clearly demonstrated their ability to inhibit KDM1A in THP-1 cells and to affect the proliferation in different leukemia cell lines. Moreover, both 49 and 50 remarkably inhibit colony formation in THP-1 human leukemia cells. The interesting biological data of this new series of reversible inhibitors prompted us to bring thienopyrroles toward further development.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    General Procedures

    Melting points were determined on a BUCHI melting point B-540 and are uncorrected. Reagents and solvents used, unless stated otherwise, were of commercially available reagent grade quality and were used without further purification. Reaction solvents were used anhydrous, purchased in Sure/Seal bottles stored over molecular sieves. Silica gel flash chromatography purifications were performed on Merck silica gel 60 (0.04–0.063 mm) or Biotage Isolera One system using prepacked columns from Grace and Biotage. Nuclear magnetic resonance spectra (1H NMR) were recorded on an Agilent VNMRS 500 MHz spectrometer at 300 K and are referenced in ppm (δ) relative to TMS. Coupling constants (J) are expressed in hertz (Hz). HPLC-MS experiments were performed on an Acquity UPLC apparatus, equipped with a diode array and a Micromass SQD single quadrupole (Waters). The compounds used for biological tests were monitored at 220 and 254 nM with two different methods, and their purity was found to be at least 95%. Semipreparative HPLC was performed on a Waters apparatus, eluting the sample through a BEH C 18 column (5 μm, 19 mm × 100 mm) using decreasingly polar mixtures of water (containing 5% of MeCN and 0.1% of trifluoroacetic acid) and MeCN (containing 0.1% of trifluoroacetic acid).

    4-Methylthieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxylate (3c)

    First, 0.92 g (38 mmol) of LiOH in 11 mL of H2O was added at rt to a solution of 1.6 g (7.7 mmol) of ethyl 4-methylthieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxylate (3b, see compound 105a in Part 1(29)) in 11 mL of EtOH. The mixture was stirred for 30 min at reflux. The solvent was evaporated then H2O was added and the solution was brought to a pH value of about 2 with 2 M HCl. The mixture was extracted with EtOAc, the combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, and the solvent was evaporated to give 1.39 g of 3c (99%) as a beige solid. 1H NMR (CDCl3) δ (ppm): 12.45 (bs, 1 H), 7.54 (d, J = 5.4 Hz, 1 H), 7.20 (d, J = 5.4 Hz, 1 H), 7.12 (s, 1 H), 3.99 (s, 3 H). MS (ESI): m/z 182 [M + H]+.

    General Procedure for the Synthesis of Compounds 3–21 and the Intermediates 22f–28f

    First, 0.24 g (2.0 mmol, 0.15 mL) of SOCl2 and 3 drops of dry DMF were added at rt to a solution of 0.277 g (1.53 mmol) of 4-methylthieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxylic acid (3c) in 9 mL of dry THF. Then the mixture was stirred for 2 h under reflux. The reaction mixture was then cooled down to rt and added to a solution of 1.3 mmol of aniline in 7 mL of pyridine. The mixture was stirred for 30 min at rt and for a further 45 min at 60 °C. Then the mixture was diluted with DCM, washed with a saturated NaHCO3 solution and brine, dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and concentrated to give an oil which was purified by flash chromatography.

    4-[(2-Nitrophenyl)methoxy]phenol (15i)

    A mixture of 6.6 g (30 mmol) of 1-(bromomethyl)-2-nitro-benzene 15g 16.5 g (150 mmol) of hydroquinone (15h) and 6.2 g (45 mmol) of K2CO3 in 110 mL acetone was heated at reflux until disappearance of the starting material.     1-Methyl-4-[4-[(3-nitrophenyl)methoxy]phenoxy]piperidine (16d)

    Five mL of 4 M HCl in 1,4-dioxane was added to a solution of 0.655 g (1.53 mmol) of tert-butyl 4-[4-[(3-nitrophenyl)methoxy]phenoxy]piperidine-1-carboxylate (16j) in 4 mL of dry 1,4-dioxane, and the mixture was stirred at rt for 90 min. The combined organic phases were dried over Na2SO4 and concentrated to afford 0.49 g (98%) of 4-[4-[(3-nitrophenyl)methoxy]phenoxy]piperidine as a yellow oil. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 8.29 (s, 1 H), 8.22–8.14 (m, 1 H), 7.92–7.83 (m, 1 H), 7.73–7.66 (m, 1 H), 6.99–6.82 (m, 4 H), 5.19 (s, 2 H), 4.29–4.16 (m, 1 H), 2.97–2.84 (m, 2 H), 2.57–2.50 (m, 2 H), 1.90–1.79 (m, 2 H), 1.47–1.33 (m, 2 H).     General Procedure to Reduce Nitro Group to Aniline

    First, 1.50 mmol of nitro derivative was dissolved in 60 mL of EtOH. Then the solution was hydrogenated in an H-Cube apparatus (PtO2 cartridge, atmospheric pressure, 70 °C, flow 0.5 mL/min). The solvent was evaporated to give the desired aniline.

    General Procedure for the Synthesis of 22–28 (BOC-Deprotection)

    General Procedure for the Synthesis of 33f–43f (Mitzunobu Reaction)

    First, 0.075 g (0.35 mmol) of diisopropyl azodicarboxylate was added dropwise to a solution of 0.089 g (0.24 mmol) of N-[2-[(4-hydroxyphenoxy)methyl]phenyl]-4-methyl-thieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxamide (33l), 0.35 mmol of the required alchol, and 0.094 g (0.35 mmol) of triphenylphosphine in 3 mL of dry THF cooled down to 0 °C and under N2 atmosphere. Then the reaction mixture was allowed to reach rt and was stirred overnight. The solvent was evaporated and the crude mixture was purified by flash chromatography (eluent n-hexane/acetone, from 0% to 11% of acetone). Trituration in Et2O provided the desired compound.

    General Procedure for the Synthesis of 33–43 (BOC-Deprotection)

    4- Methyl-N-[2-[[4-(4-piperidylmethoxy)phenoxy]methyl]phenyl]thieno[3,2-b]pyrrole-5-carboxamide (38)

    First, 0.14 mmol of the boc-protected derivative was dissolved in 1 mL of dry 1,4-dioxane. Then 0.80 mL of 4 M HCl in 1,4-dioxane was added and the mixture was stirred at rt for 2 h. The mixture was then concentrated, and the residue was taken up in a saturated NaHCO3 solution and extracted with DCM. The combined organic phases were dried over Na2SO4, filtered, the solvent removed, and the product was purified by silica gel flash chromatography (eluent DCM/MeOH/NH3, 94:6:0.6, v:v:v). Trituration in Et2O provided the desired compounds

    Methyl 2-(Methoxymethyl)-6-nitro-benzoate (45v)

    First, 1.621 g (9.05 mmol) of 7-nitro-3H-isobenzofuran-1-one was suspended in 9.5 mL of 1 M NaOH and 5 mL of THF. Then the mixture was heated at 50 °C for 30 min and the solution evaporated under vacuum to give sodium 2-(hydroxymethyl)-6-nitro-benzoate (45n) as a white powder. Then 0.43 g (11 mmol) of 60% NaH (mineral oil) were added to a suspension of 45n in DMA at 0 °C, and the mixture was allowed to spontaneously warm to rt. After 30 min, 3.85 g (27.2 mmol) of iodomethane were added at 0 °C and the mixture was stirred at rt overnight. Water was added, and the mixture extracted with EtOAc, washed with brine, dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and evaporated to give 2.25 g of crude which was purified by silica gel column chromatography (eluent n-hexane/EtOAc) to yield 971 mg (48%) of 45v as a white powder. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 8.15–7.71 (m, 3 H), 4.51 (s, 2 H), 3.84 (s, 3 H), 3.26 (s, 3 H). MS (ESI): m/z 248 [M + 23]+.

    Yield 46% (131 mg), pale-yellow oil. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 8.14–7.70 (m, 3 H), 4.55 (s, 2 H), 4.34–4.27 (m, 2 H), 3.46–3.40 (m, 2 H), 1.31–1.06 (m, 6 H).

    [2-(Methoxymethyl)-6-nitro-phenyl]methanol (45w)

    A solution of 0.84 g (3.7 mmol) of methyl 2-(methoxymethyl)-6-nitro-benzoate (45v) in 3 mL of dry THF was added to a stirred suspension of 9.3 mL (18.6 mmol) of 2 M lithium borohydride in THF at 0 °C under nitrogen, followed by 2 mL of dry MeOH, and the mixture was stirred 3 h at rt. The reaction was cooled to 0 °C, treated with a saturated aqueous NH4Cl solution, and extracted with EtOAc. The combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and evaporated to give the crude product which was purified by flash chromatography (eluent n-hexane/EtOAc) to yield 589 mg (80%) of 45w as a white solid. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 7.75–7.63 (m, 2 H), 7.52–7.45 (m, 1 H), 5.29–5.24 (m, 1 H), 4.64–4.61 (m, 2 H), 4.58 (s, 2 H), 3.33 (s, 3 H). MS (ESI): m/z 198 [M + H]+.

    2-(Bromomethyl)-1-(methoxymethyl)-3-nitro-benzene (45g)

    To a stirred solution of 556 mg (2.82 mmol) of [2-(methoxymethyl)-6-nitro-phenyl]methanol (45w) in 1.3 mL of dry acetonitrile, 1.4 g (4.2 mmol) of carbon tetrabromide and 1.3 g (5.1 mmol) of triphenylphosphine were added and the mixture stirred at rt for 1 h. Water was added, solvent evaporated, and the aqueous mixture extracted with EtOAc. The combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and evaporated to give the crude which was purified by flash chromatography (eluent n-hexane/EtOAc) to yield 623 mg (85%) of 45g as a pale-yellow oil. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 7.98–7.75 (2m, 2 H), 7.65–7.59 (m, 1 H), 4.81 (s, 2 H), 4.66 (s, 2 H), 3.33 (s, 3 H). MS (ESI): m/z 261 [M + H]+.

    4-[[2-(Methoxymethyl)-6-nitro-phenyl]methoxy]phenol (45l)

    First, 0.620 g (2.39 mmol) of 2-(bromomethyl)-1-(methoxymethyl)-3-nitro-benzene (45g), 1.31 g (11.93 mmol) of hydroquinone, and 0.49 g (3.6 mmol) of K2CO3 were suspended in 10 mL of acetone and refluxed in a sealed vial for 2.5 h. Solvent was removed under vacuum, and the residue was portioned between DCM and water. The aqueous phase was extracted with DCM, washed with brine, dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and evaporated to give the crude (700 mg) which was purified by flash chromatography (eluent DCM/MeOH 99:1) to yield 519 mg (79%) of 45l as a dark oil. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 9.01 (s, 1 H), 7.87–7.71 (m, 2 H), 7.65–7.54 (m, 1 H), 6.81–6.62 (2m, 4 H), 5.11 (s, 2 H), 4.60 (s, 2 H), 3.32 (s, 3 H).     tert-Butyl 4-[4-[[2-(Methoxymethyl)-6-nitro-phenyl]methoxy]phenoxy]piperidine-1-carboxylate (45d)

    First, 0.14 g (0.52 mmol) of triphenyl phosphine were added to a stirred solution of 0.1 g (0.346 mmol) of 4-[[2-(methoxymethyl)-6-nitro-phenyl]methoxy]phenol (45l) and 0.10 g (0.52 mmol) of tert-butyl 4-hydroxypiperidine-1-carboxylate in 2 mL of dry THF under nitrogen, at rt.     Isopropyl 2-(Hydroxymethyl)-6-nitrobenzoate (52o)
    To a stirred solution of 0.734 g (3.35 mmol) of sodium 2-(hydroxymethyl)-6-nitrobenzoate (45n) in 3 mL of DMA, 0.598 g (3.52 mmol) of 2-iodopropane was added and the mixture stirred at rt for 12 h. A further portion of 0.067 mL of 2-iodopropane (0.67 mmol) was added, and stirring was continued at rt for 16 h. Water was added to the mixture and the aqueous phase extracted with EtOAc; the combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and evaporated to dryness. The residue was purified by flash chromatography (eluent n-hexane/EtOAc) to give 388 mg (48%) of 52o as a pale-yellow solid. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 8.11–7.67 (m, 3 H), 5.59 (br s, 1 H), 5.22–5.09 (m, 1 H), 4.58 (m, 2 H), 1.28 (m, 6 H).
    Isopropyl 2-[[tert-Butyl(dimethyl)silyl]oxymethyl]-6-nitrobenzoate (52p)
    First, 0.27 g (1.8 mmol) of tert-butyl-chloro-dimethylsilane dissolved in 1 mL of DCM was added under nitrogen at 0 °C to a solution of 0.387 g (1.62 mmol) of isopropyl 2-(hydroxymethyl)-6-nitro-benzoate (52o) and 0.14 g (2.1 mmol) of imidazole in 1 mL of dry DCM. Then the mixture was stirred for 15 min; the white precipitate was filtered off and rinsed with DCM providing 572 mg (quantitative) of 52p as a pale-yellow oil. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 8.11–7.73 (m, 3 H), 5.21–5.11 (m, 1 H), 4.77 (s, 2 H), 1.31–1.27 (m, 6 H), 0.87 (s, 9 H), 0.06 (s, 6 H). MS (ESI): m/z 354 [M + H]+.
    [2-[[tert-Butyl(dimethyl)silyl]oxymethyl]-6-nitro-phenyl]methanol (52q)
    A solution of 0.594 g (1.68 mmol) of isopropyl 2-[[tert-butyl(dimethyl)silyl]oxymethyl]-6-nitrobenzoate (52p) in 1.7 mL of THF was added to 2.7 mL (5.4 mmol) of 2 M LiBH4 in THF at 0 °C under nitrogen. Then, 0.24 mL of dry MeOH were added and the mixture was stirred at 60 °C for 30 min, cooled down to 0 °C, quenched with a saturated aqueous NH4Cl solution, and extracted with EtOAc. The combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and evaporated to afford a residue, which was purified by flash chromatography (eluent n-hexane EtOAc from 1% to 10% of EtOAc), providing 303 mg (61%) of 52q as a pale-yellow oil. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 7.77–7.44 (m, 3 H), 5.28–5.23 (m, 1 H), 4.88 (s, 2 H), 4.63–4.59 (m, 2 H), 0.90 (s, 9 H), 0.09 (s, 6 H). MS (ESI): m/z 298 [M + H]+.
    [2-[[tert-Butyl(dimethyl)silyl]oxymethyl]-6-nitro-phenyl]methylmethanesulfonate (52r)
    First, 0.17 mL (1.2 mmol) of TEA were added under nitrogen to a solution of 0.28 g (0.94 mmol) of [2-[[tert-butyl(dimethyl)silyl]oxymethyl]-6-nitro-phenyl]methanol (52q) in 3 mL of DCM at −15 °C, followed by 0.087 mL (1.1 mmol) of methanesulfonyl chloride. The mixture was stirred for 20 min at −15 °C, quenched with a saturated aqueous NaHCO3 solution, and extracted with DCM. The combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and evaporated to afford 350 mg (quantitative) of 52r as a yellow oil. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6) δ (ppm): 7.95–7.65 (m, 3 H), 5.43 (s, 2 H), 4.93 (s, 2 H), 3.23 (s, 3 H), 0.90 (s, 9 H), 0.1 (s, 6 H).

    Protein Preparation, Crystallization, and Structure Determination

    The KDM1A/CoREST complex was expressed and purified as previously reported.(34) The complex, concentrated at approximately 7 mg/mL, was crystallized in hanging drops in 24-well plates at 20 °C, mixing 1 μL of protein complex with 1 μL of reservoir precipitant solution (1.1–1.2 M Na/K tartrate, 0.1 M ADA, pH 6.0). Crystals were soaked with the different compounds by adding to the drops 1 μL of 1 mM inhibitor solution, prepared by diluting the 20 mM stock of inhibitor in 100% DMSO in 1.6 M Na/K tartrate, 10% glycerol, 0.1 M ADA, pH 6.0. After ca. 1–2 h, crystals were transferred into 1 mM solution of inhibitor in 1.6 M Na/K tartrate, 10% glycerol, 0.1 M ADA, pH 6.0, and after few seconds or some minutes they were flash frozen in liquid nitrogen. Diffraction patterns from crystals soaked with different compounds were collected at beamline X06DA/PXIII of the Swiss Light Source (SLS, Paul Sherrer Institut, Villigen, Switzerland) or ID30A-1/Massif-1 of the European Synchrotron Radiation Facility (ESRF, Grenoble, France) at 100 K. Diffraction data were processed with XDS/XSCALE(38) within the automated data reduction system xia2.(39) The structures were solved by molecular replacement with Phaser(40) within the Phenix suite,(41) using the protein chains from PDB entry 2V1D as search model. The models were improved by iterative cycles of manual building in Coot(42) and refinement with phenix.refine.(43) Inhibitors were automatically placed with the phenix.ligandfit procedure(44) in Phenix, and the structures were then further refined. Crystallographic statistics are reported in the Supporting Information.

    Molecular Modeling Activities

    Pose production, clustering, and scoring were implemented within the architecture described in our recent work.(45) Protein targets have been prepared and used according to the procedures described in Supporting Information.

    Biology

    Material and methods for biochemical and cellular assays have been included in Supporting Information.

      The role of histone demethylases in cancer therapy
      Hoffmann, Inga; Roatsch, Martin; Schmitt, Martin L.; Carlino, Luca; Pippel, Martin; Sippl, Wolfgang; Jung, Manfred
      Molecular Oncology (2012), 6 (6), 683-703CODEN: MOONC3; ISSN:1574-7891. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. Reversible histone methylation has emerged in the last few years as an important mechanism of epigenetic regulation. Histone methyltransferases and demethylases have been identified as contributing factors in the development of several diseases, esp. cancer. Therefore, they have been postulated to be new drug targets with high therapeutic potential. Here, we review histone demethylases with a special focus on their potential role in oncol. drug discovery. We present an overview over the different classes of enzymes, their biochem., selected data on their role in physiol. and already available inhibitors.

      2
      Histone demethylation mediated by the nuclear amine oxidase homolog LSD1
      Shi, Yujiang; Lan, Fei; Matson, Caitlin; Mulligan, Peter; Whetstine, Johnathan R.; Cole, Philip A.; Casero, Robert A.; Shi, Yang
      Cell (Cambridge, MA, United States) (2004), 119 (7), 941-953CODEN: CELLB5; ISSN:0092-8674. (Cell Press)
      Posttranslational modifications of histone N-terminal tails impact chromatin structure and gene transcription. While the extent of histone acetylation is detd. by both acetyltransferases and deacetylases, it has been unclear whether histone methylation is also regulated by enzymes with opposing activities. Here, we provide evidence that LSD1 (KIAA0601), a nuclear homolog of amine oxidases, functions as a histone demethylase and transcriptional corepressor. LSD1 specifically demethylates histone H3 lysine 4, which is linked to active transcription. Lysine demethylation occurs via an oxidn. reaction that generates formaldehyde. Importantly, RNAi inhibition of LSD1 causes an increase in H3 lysine 4 methylation and concomitant derepression of target genes, suggesting that LSD1 represses transcription via histone demethylation. The results thus identify a histone demethylase conserved from S. pombe to human and reveal dynamic regulation of histone methylation by both histone methylases and demethylases.

      Karytinos, A.; Forneris, F.; Profumo, A.; Ciossani, G.; Battaglioli, E.; Binda, C.; Mattevi, A. A Novel Mammalian Flavin-Dependent Histone Demethylase J. Biol. Chem. 2009, 284, 17775– 17782 DOI: 10.1074/jbc.M109.003087
      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar

      A Novel Mammalian Flavin-dependent Histone Demethylase
      Karytinos, Aristotele; Forneris, Federico; Profumo, Antonella; Ciossani, Giuseppe; Battaglioli, Elena; Binda, Claudia; Mattevi, Andrea
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (2009), 284 (26), 17775-17782CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      Methylation of Lys residues on histone proteins is a well known and extensively characterized epigenetic mark. The recent discovery of lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1) demonstrated that lysine methylation can be dynamically controlled. Among the histone demethylases so far identified, LSD1 has the unique feature of functioning through a flavin-dependent amine oxidn. reaction. Data base anal. reveals that mammalian genomes contain a gene (AOF1, for amine-oxidase flavin-contg. domain 1) that is homologous to the LSD1-coding gene. Here, we demonstrate that the protein encoded by AOF1 represents a second mammalian flavin-dependent histone demethylase, named LSD2. The new demethylase is strictly specific for mono- and dimethylated Lys4 of histone H3, recognizes a long stretch of the H3 N-terminal tail, senses the presence of addnl. epigenetic marks on the histone substrate, and is covalently inhibited by tranylcypromine. As opposed to LSD1, LSD2 does not form a biochem. stable complex with the C-terminal domain of the corepressor protein CoREST. Furthermore, LSD2 contains a CW-type zinc finger motif with potential zinc-binding sites that are not present in LSD1. We conclude that mammalian LSD2 represents a new flavin-dependent H3-Lys4 demethylase that features substrate specificity properties highly similar to those of LSD1 but is very likely to be part of chromatin-remodeling complexes that are distinct from those involving LSD1.

      LSD1 inhibition: a therapeutic strategy in cancer?
      Lynch, James T.; Harris, William J.; Somervaille, Tim C. P.
      Expert Opinion on Therapeutic Targets (2012), 16 (12), 1239-1249CODEN: EOTTAO; ISSN:1472-8222. (Informa Healthcare)
      A review. Introduction: The role of epigenetic dysfunction in cancer is increasingly appreciated. This has raised the question as to whether enzymes that regulate the structure and function of chromatin might represent novel therapeutic targets. The histone demethylase LSD1 is one such candidate and novel, potent inhibitors are under development.Areas covered: The literature on LSD1 (also known as KDM1A, AOF2, BHC110 or KIAA0601) was identified in Pubmed and is herein discussed. Areas covered include the structure and enzymic activity of LSD1, its role in chromatin regulatory complexes, its functional roles in normal and malignant tissue, pharmacol. inhibitors of its activity and their putative therapeutic roles.Expert opinion: Pre-clin. data supporting a therapeutic role for LSD1 inhibitors are most encouraging in acute myeloid leukemia, although optimal dosing strategies and beneficial combinations with other agents remain unclear. Studies making use of potent, selective LSD1 inhibitors active in the nanomolar range are required to establish therapeutic indications in other subtypes of haematol. malignancy, and in solid tumors.

      LSD1 demethylates repressive histone marks to promote androgen-receptor-dependent transcription
      Metzger, Eric; Wissmann, Melanie; Yin, Na; Mueller, Judith M.; Schneider, Robert; Peters, Antoine H. F. M.; Guenther, Thomas; Buettner, Reinhard; Schuele, Roland
      Nature (London, United Kingdom) (2005), 437 (7057), 436-439CODEN: NATUAS; ISSN:0028-0836. (Nature Publishing Group)
      Gene regulation in eukaryotes requires the coordinate interaction of chromatin-modulating proteins with specific transcription factors such as the androgen receptor. Gene activation and repression is specifically regulated by histone methylation status at distinct lysine residues. Here the authors show that lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1; also known as BHC110) co-localizes with the androgen receptor in normal human prostate and prostate tumor. LSD1 interacts with androgen receptor in vitro and in vivo, and stimulates androgen-receptor-dependent transcription. Conversely, knockdown of LSD1 protein levels abrogates androgen-induced transcriptional activation and cell proliferation. Chromatin immunopptn. analyses demonstrate that androgen receptor and LSD1 form chromatin-assocd. complexes in a ligand-dependent manner. LSD1 relieves repressive histone marks by demethylation of histone H3 at lysine 9 (H3-K9), thereby leading to de-repression of androgen receptor target genes. Furthermore, the authors identify pargyline as an inhibitor of LSD1. Pargyline blocks demethylation of H3-K9 by LSD1 and consequently androgen-receptor-dependent transcription. Thus, modulation of LSD1 activity offers a new strategy to regulate androgen receptor functions. Here, the authors link demethylation of a repressive histone mark with androgen-receptor-dependent gene activation, thus providing a mechanism by which demethylases control specific gene expression.

      Lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1) is highly expressed in ER-negative breast cancers and a biomarker predicting aggressive biology
      Lim, Soyoung; Janzer, Andreas; Becker, Astrid; Zimmer, Andreas; Schuele, Roland; Buettner, Reinhard; Kirfel, Jutta
      Carcinogenesis (2010), 31 (3), 512-520CODEN: CRNGDP; ISSN:0143-3334. (Oxford University Press)
      Breast carcinogenesis is a multistep process involving both genetic and epigenetic changes. Since epigenetic changes like histone modifications are potentially reversible processes, much effort has been directed toward understanding this mechanism with the goal of finding novel therapies as well as more refined diagnostic and prognostic tools in breast cancer. Lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1) plays a key role in the regulation of gene expression by removing the Me groups from methylated lysine 4 of histone H3 and lysine 9 of histone H3. LSD1 is essential for mammalian development and involved in many biol. processes. Considering recent evidence that LSD1 is involved in carcinogenesis, we investigated the role of LSD1 in breast cancer. Therefore, we developed an ELISA to det. LSD1 protein levels in tissue specimens of breast cancer and measured very high LSD1 levels in estrogen receptor (ER)-neg. tumors. Pharmacol. LSD1 inhibition resulted in growth inhibition of breast cancer cells. Knockdown of LSD1 using small interfering RNA approach induced regulation of several proliferation-assocd. genes like p21, ERBB2 and CCNA2. Addnl., we found that LSD1 is recruited to the promoters of these genes. In summary, our data indicate that LSD1 may provide a predictive marker for aggressive biol. and a novel attractive therapeutic target for treatment of ER-neg. breast cancers.

      Lysine-specific demethylase 1 is strongly expressed in poorly differentiated neuroblastoma: Implications for therapy
      Schulte, Johannes H.; Lim, Soyoung; Schramm, Alexander; Friedrichs, Nicolaus; Koster, Jan; Versteeg, Rogier; Ora, Ingrid; Pajtler, Kristian; Klein-Hitpass, Ludger; Kuhfittig-Kulle, Steffi; Metzger, Eric; Schuele, Roland; Eggert, Angelika; Buettner, Reinhard; Kirfel, Jutta
      Cancer Research (2009), 69 (5), 2065-2071CODEN: CNREA8; ISSN:0008-5472. (American Association for Cancer Research)
      Aberrant epigenetic changes in DNA methylation and histone acetylation are hallmarks of most cancers, whereas histone methylation was previously considered to be irreversible and less versatile. Recently, several histone demethylases were identified catalyzing the removal of Me groups from histone H3 lysine residues and thereby influencing gene expression. Neuroblastomas continue to remain a clin. challenge despite advances in multimodal therapy. Here, we address the functional significance of the chromatin-modifying enzyme lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1) in neuroblastoma. LSD1 expression correlated with adverse outcome and was inversely correlated with differentiation in neuroblastic tumors. Differentiation of neuroblastoma cells resulted in down-regulation of LSD1. Small interfering RNA-mediated knockdown of LSD1 decreased cellular growth, induced expression of differentiation-assocd. genes, and increased target gene-specific H3K4 methylation. Moreover, LSD1 inhibition using monoamine oxidase inhibitors resulted in an increase of global H3K4 methylation and growth inhibition of neuroblastoma cells in vitro. Finally, targeting LSD1 reduced neuroblastoma xenograft growth in vivo. Here, we provide the first evidence that a histone demethylase, LSD1, is involved in maintaining the undifferentiated, malignant phenotype of neuroblastoma cells. We show that inhibition of LSD1 reprograms the transcriptome of neuroblastoma cells and inhibits neuroblastoma xenograft growth. Our results suggest that targeting histone demethylases may provide a novel option for cancer therapy.

      Histone H3 Lysine 4 Demethylation Is a Target of Nonselective Antidepressive Medications
      Lee, Min Gyu; Wynder, Christopher; Schmidt, Dawn M.; McCafferty, Dewey G.; Shiekhattar, Ramin
      Chemistry & Biology (Cambridge, MA, United States) (2006), 13 (6), 563-567CODEN: CBOLE2; ISSN:1074-5521. (Cell Press)
      Demethylation of histone H3 lysine 4 is carried out by BHC110/LSD1, an enzyme with close homol. to monoamine oxidases (MAO). Monoamine oxidase A or B are frequent targets of selective and nonselective small mol. inhibitors used for treatment of depression. Here the authors show that in contrast to selective monoamine oxidase inhibitors such as pargyline, nonselective monoamine oxidase inhibitors potently inhibit nucleosomal demethylation of histone H3 lysine 4. Tranylcypromine (brand name Parnate) displayed the best inhibitory activity with an IC50 of less than 2 μM. Treatment of P19 embryonal carcinoma cells with tranylcypromine resulted in global increase in H3K4 methylation as well as transcriptional derepression of two BHC110 target genes, Egr1 and the pluripotent stem cell marker Oct4. These results attest to the effectiveness of tranylcypromine as a small mol. inhibitor of histone demethylation.

      An Overview of Phenylcyclopropylamine Derivatives: Biochemical and Biological Significance and Recent Developments
      Khan, Mohammed Naseer Ahmed; Suzuki, Takayoshi; Miyata, Naoki
      Medicinal Research Reviews (2013), 33 (4), 873-910CODEN: MRREDD; ISSN:0198-6325. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
      A review. Trans-2-Phencylcyclopropylamine (2-PCPA), a potent, clin. used antidepressant, affects monoamine neurotransmitter levels by inhibiting the main metabolizing enzymes, monoamine oxidases (MAOs). However, the antidepressant action of this compd. was not fully explained by its effects on MAOs due to its wide variety of biol. effects. 2-PCPA also affects depression-assocd. pathophysiol. pathways, and linked with increased levels of trace amines in brain, upregulation of GABAB receptors (where GABA is gamma amino butyric acid), modulation of phospholipid metab., and interference with various cytochrome P 450 (CYP) enzymes. Consequently, despite its adverse effects and limited clin. applicability, 2-PCPA has attracted interest as a structural scaffold for the development of mechanism-based inhibitors of various enzymes, including lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1), which is a possible target for cancer chemotherapy. In the recent years, many reports have appeared in the literature based on 2-PCPA scaffold and their potential medicinal implications. This review mainly focuses on the medicinal chem. aspects including drug design, structure-activity relationships (SAR), biol. and biochem. properties, and mechanism of actions of 2-PCPA and its derivs. Furthermore, we also highlight recent advance in this area and discuss their future applications for beneficial therapeutic effects.

      Lysine-specific demethylase 1-selective inactivators: Protein-targeted drug delivery mechanism
      Ogasawara, Daisuke; Itoh, Yukihiro; Tsumoto, Hiroki; Kakizawa, Taeko; Mino, Koshiki; Fukuhara, Kiyoshi; Nakagawa, Hidehiko; Hasegawa, Makoto; Sasaki, Ryuzo; Mizukami, Tamio; Miyata, Naoki; Suzuki, Takayoshi
      Angewandte Chemie, International Edition (2013), 52 (33), 8620-8624CODEN: ACIEF5; ISSN:1433-7851. (Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA)
      A series of novel LSD1 (LSD1 = lysine-specific demethylase) inactivators based on new concept of LSD1-targeted PCPA (PCPA = trans-2-phenylcyclopropylamine) delivery have been designed. This concept was tested with peptide 1a [1a = (ART-X-QTARKSTGGKAPRKQLA)] X = (I) and confirmed that 1a does indeed inhibit LSD1 selectively and efficiently by delivering PCPA directly to the LSD1 active site. The enzyme is then inactivated by FAD-PCPA adduct formation, as in the case of PCPA itself. This strategy could be applied to the design of nonpeptide, small-mol. LSD1 inactivators. Mechanistic studies and biol. evaluations revealed that these small-mol. compds. are potent LSD1-selective inactivators (comparable to peptide 1a) in enzyme assays, and they also exhibit potent cell growth inhibitory activities against cancer cell lines. This is the first time that a drug-carrier-type system has been employed to target a specific mol. These novel LSD1-selective inactivators are considered to be candidates for anticancer agents, as well as bioprobes.

      Advances in the development of histone lysine demethylase inhibitors
      Maes, Tamara; Carceller, Elena; Salas, Jordi; Ortega, Alberto; Buesa, Carlos
      Current Opinion in Pharmacology (2015), 23 (), 52-60CODEN: COPUBK; ISSN:1471-4892. (Elsevier Ltd.)
      A review. The covalent modification of histones is closely assocd. with regulation of gene transcription. Chromatin modifications have been suggested to represent an epigenetic code that is dynamically 'written' and 'erased' by specialized proteins, and 'read', or interpreted, by proteins that translate the code into gene expression changes. Initially thought to be an irreversible process, histone methylation is now known to be reversed by demethylases, FAD dependent amineoxidases and by iron(II)-alpha-ketoglutarate dependent deoxygenases of the Jumonji family. Altered histone demethylase activities have been assocd. with human disease, including cancer. The first wave of novel investigational drugs directed against KDM1A has recently entered the clinic, and the first specific inhibitor targeting a Jumonji KDM is advancing in preclin. regulatory studies.

      Reversible Inhibitors of LSD1 as Therapeutic Agents in Acute Myeloid Leukemia: Clinical Significance and Progress to Date
      Mould, Daniel P.; McGonagle, Alison E.; Wiseman, Daniel H.; Williams, Emma L.; Jordan, Allan M.
      Medicinal Research Reviews (2015), 35 (3), 586-618CODEN: MRREDD; ISSN:0198-6325. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)

Overall, while the development of reversible inhibitors to date has been less fruitful than that of irreversible inhibitors, there is still the possibility for their use to facilitate further research into the roles and functions of LSD1 and to expand the therapeutic applications of LSD1 inhibitors in the clinic.

      3-(Piperidin-4-ylmethoxy)pyridine Containing Compounds Are Potent Inhibitors of Lysine Specific Demethylase 1
      Wu, Fangrui; Zhou, Chao; Yao, Yuan; Wei, Liping; Feng, Zizhen; Deng, Lisheng; Song, Yongcheng

      Reversible LSD1 Inhibition Interferes with Global EWS/ETS Transcriptional Activity and Impedes Ewing Sarcoma Tumor Growth
      Sankar, Savita; Theisen, Emily R.; Bearss, Jared; Mulvihill, Timothy; Hoffman, Laura M.; Sorna, Venkataswamy; Beckerle, Mary C.; Sharma, Sunil; Lessnick, Stephen L.
      Clinical Cancer Research (2014), 20 (17), 4584-4597CODEN: CCREF4; ISSN:1078-0432. (American Association for Cancer Research)
      Purpose: Ewing sarcoma is a pediatric bone tumor that absolutely relies on the transcriptional activity of the EWS/ETS family of fusion oncoproteins. While the most common fusion, EWS/FLI, utilizes lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1) to repress crit. tumor suppressors, small-mol. blockade of LSD1 has not yet been thoroughly explored as a therapeutic approach for Ewing sarcoma. We therefore evaluated the translational potential of potent and specific LSD1 inhibition with HCI2509 on the transcriptional program of both EWS/FLI and EWS/ERG as well as the downstream oncogenic phenotypes driven by EWS/ETS fusions in both in vitro and in vivo models of Ewing sarcoma. Exptl. Design: RNA-seq was used to compare the transcriptional profiles of EWS/FLI, EWS/ERG, and treatment with HCI2509 in both EWS/FLI- and EWS/ERG-contg. cell lines. We then evaluated morphol. phenotypes of treated cells with immunofluorescence. The induction of apoptosis was evaluated using caspase-3/7 activation and TUNEL staining. Colony forming assays were used to test oncogenic transformation and xenograft studies with patient-derived cell lines were used to evaluate the effects of HCI2509 on tumorigenesis. Results: HCI2509 caused a dramatic reversal of both the up- and downregulated transcriptional profiles of EWS/FLI and EWS/ERG accompanied by the induction of apoptosis and disruption of morphol. and oncogenic phenotypes modulated by EWS/FLI. Importantly, HCI2509 displayed single-agent efficacy in multiple xenograft models. Conclusions: These data support epigenetic modulation with HCI2509 as a therapeutic strategy for Ewing sarcoma, and highlight a crit. dual role for LSD1 in the oncogenic transcriptional activity of EWS/ETS proteins.

      Structural Basis of LSD1-CoREST Selectivity in Histone H3 Recognition
      Forneris, Federico; Binda, Claudia; Adamo, Antonio; Battaglioli, Elena; Mattevi, Andrea
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (2007), 282 (28), 20070-20074CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      Histone demethylase LSD1 regulates transcription by demethylating Lys4 of histone H3. The crystal structure of the enzyme in complex with CoREST and a substrate-like peptide inhibitor highlights an intricate network of interactions and a folded conformation of the bound peptide. The core of the peptide structure is formed by Arg2, Gln5, and Ser10, which are engaged in specific intramol. H-bonds. Several charged side chains on the surface of the substrate-binding pocket establish electrostatic interactions with the peptide. The three-dimensional structure predicts that methylated Lys4 binds in a solvent inaccessible position in front of the flavin cofactor. This geometry is fully consistent with the demethylation reaction being catalyzed through a flavin-mediated oxidn. of the substrate amino-Me group. These features dictate the exquisite substrate specificity of LSD1 and provide a structural framework to explain the fine tuning of its catalytic activity and the active role of CoREST in substrate recognition.

      Kerenyi, Marc A.; Shao, Zhen; Hsu, Yu-Jung; Guo, Guoji; Luc, Sidinh; O'Brien, Kassandra; Fujiwara, Yuko; Peng, Cong; Nguyen, Minh; Orkin, Stuart H.

      Phaser crystallographic software
      McCoy, Airlie J.; Grosse-Kunstleve, Ralf W.; Adams, Paul D.; Winn, Martyn D.; Storoni, Laurent C.; Read, Randy J.
      Journal of Applied Crystallography (2007), 40 (4), 658-674CODEN: JACGAR; ISSN:0021-8898. (International Union of Crystallography)
      Phaser is a program for phasing macromol. crystal structures by both mol. replacement and exptl. phasing methods. The novel phasing algorithms implemented in Phaser have been developed using max. likelihood and multivariate statistics. For mol. replacement, the new algorithms have proved to be significantly better than traditional methods in discriminating correct solns. from noise, and for single-wavelength anomalous dispersion exptl. phasing, the new algorithms, which account for correlations between F+ and F-, give better phases (lower mean phase error with respect to the phases given by the refined structure) than those that use mean F and anomalous differences ΔF. One of the design concepts of Phaser was that it be capable of a high degree of automation. To this end, Phaser (written in C++) can be called directly from Python, although it can also be called using traditional CCP4 keyword-style input. Phaser is a platform for future development of improved phasing methods and their release, including source code, to the crystallog. community.

      PHENIX: a comprehensive Python-based system for macromolecular structure solution
      Adams, Paul D.; Afonine, Pavel V.; Bunkoczi, Gabor; Chen, Vincent B.; Davis, Ian W.; Echols, Nathaniel; Headd, Jeffrey J.; Hung, Li Wei; Kapral, Gary J.; Grosse-Kunstleve, Ralf W.; McCoy, Airlie J.; Moriarty, Nigel W.; Oeffner, Robert; Read, Randy J.; Richardson, David C.; Richardson, Jane S.; Terwilliger, Thomas C.; Zwart, Peter H.
      Acta Crystallographica, Section D: Biological Crystallography (2010), 66 (2), 213-221CODEN: ABCRE6; ISSN:0907-4449. (International Union of Crystallography)
      A review. Macromol. X-ray crystallog. is routinely applied to understand biol. processes at a mol. level. However, significant time and effort are still required to solve and complete many of these structures because of the need for manual interpretation of complex numerical data using many software packages and the repeated use of interactive three-dimensional graphics. PHENIX has been developed to provide a comprehensive system for macromol. crystallog. structure soln. with an emphasis on the automation of all procedures. This has relied on the development of algorithms that minimize or eliminate subjective input, the development of algorithms that automate procedures that are traditionally performed by hand and, finally, the development of a framework that allows a tight integration between the algorithms.

      42
      Features and development of Coot
      Emsley, P.; Lohkamp, B.; Scott, W. G.; Cowtan, K.
      Acta Crystallographica, Section D: Biological Crystallography (2010), 66 (4), 486-501CODEN: ABCRE6; ISSN:0907-4449. (International Union of Crystallography)
      Coot is a mol.-graphics application for model building and validation of biol. macromols. The program displays electron-d. maps and at. models and allows model manipulations such as idealization, real-space refinement, manual rotation/translation, rigid-body fitting, ligand search, solvation, mutations, rotamers and Ramachandran idealization. Furthermore, tools are provided for model validation as well as interfaces to external programs for refinement, validation and graphics. The software is designed to be easy to learn for novice users, which is achieved by ensuring that tools for common tasks are 'discoverable' through familiar user-interface elements (menus and toolbars) or by intuitive behavior (mouse controls). Recent developments have focused on providing tools for expert users, with customisable key bindings, extensions and an extensive scripting interface. The software is under rapid development, but has already achieved very widespread use within the crystallog. community. The current state of the software is presented, with a description of the facilities available and of some of the underlying methods employed.

      43
      Towards automated crystallographic structure refinement with phenix.refine
      Afonine, Pavel V.; Grosse-Kunstleve, Ralf W.; Echols, Nathaniel; Headd, Jeffrey J.; Moriarty, Nigel W.; Mustyakimov, Marat; Terwilliger, Thomas C.; Urzhumtsev, Alexandre; Zwart, Peter H.; Adams, Paul D.
      Acta Crystallographica, Section D: Biological Crystallography (2012), 68 (4), 352-367CODEN: ABCRE6; ISSN:0907-4449. (International Union of Crystallography)
      Phenix.refine is a program within the PHENIX package that supports crystallog. structure refinement against exptl. data with a wide range of upper resoln. limits using a large repertoire of model parameterizations. It has several automation features and is also highly flexible. Several hundred parameters enable extensive customizations for complex use cases. Multiple user-defined refinement strategies can be applied to specific parts of the model in a single refinement run. An intuitive graphical user interface is available to guide novice users and to assist advanced users in managing refinement projects. X-ray or neutron diffraction data can be used sep. or jointly in refinement. phenix.refine is tightly integrated into the PHENIX suite, where it serves as a crit. component in automated model building, final structure refinement, structure validation and deposition to the wwPDB. This paper presents an overview of the major phenix.refine features, with extensive literature refs. for readers interested in more detailed discussions of the methods.

      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
      44
      Automated ligand fitting by core-fragment fitting and extension into density
      Terwilliger, Thomas C.; Klei, Herbert; Adams, Paul D.; Moriarty, Nigel W.; Cohn, Judith D.
      Acta Crystallographica, Section D: Biological Crystallography (2006), D62 (8), 915-922CODEN: ABCRE6; ISSN:0907-4449. (Blackwell Publishing Ltd.)
      A procedure for fitting of ligands to electron-d. maps by first fitting a core fragment of the ligand to d. and then extending the remainder of the ligand into d. is presented. The approach was tested by fitting 9327 ligands over a wide range of resolns. (most are in the range 0.8-4.8 Å) from the Protein Data Bank (PDB) into (Fo - Fc)exp(iφc) difference d. calcd. using entries from the PDB without these ligands. The procedure was able to place 58% of these 9327 ligands within 2 Å (r.m.s.d.) of the coordinates of the atoms in the original PDB entry for that ligand. The success of the fitting procedure was relatively insensitive to the size of the ligand in the range 10-100 non-H atoms and was only moderately sensitive to resoln., with the percentage of ligands placed near the coordinates of the original PDB entry for fits in the range 58-73% over all resoln. ranges tested.

      Software Infrastructure for Computer-aided Drug Discovery and Development, a Practical Example with Guidelines
      Moretti, Loris; Sartori, Luca
      Molecular Informatics (2016), 35 (8-9), 382-390CODEN: MIONBS; ISSN:1868-1743. (Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA)
      In the field of Computer-Aided Drug Discovery and Development (CADDD) the proper software infrastructure is essential for everyday investigations. The creation of such an environment should be carefully planned and implemented with certain features in order to be productive and efficient. Here, we describe a soln. to integrate std. computational services into a functional unit that empowers modeling applications for drug discovery. This system allows users with various level of expertise to run in silico expts. automatically and without the burden of file formatting for different software, managing the actual computation, keeping track of the activities and graphical rendering of the structural outcomes. To showcase the potential of this approach, performances of five different docking programs on an Hiv-1 protease test set are presented.

Histone methyltransferases and demethylases have been identified as contributing factors in the development of several diseases, esp. cancer. Therefore, they have been postulated to be new drug targets with high therapeutic potential. Here, we review histone demethylases with a special focus on their potential role in oncol. drug discovery.         Histone demethylation mediated by the nuclear amine oxidase homolog LSD1
        Shi, Yujiang; Lan, Fei; Matson, Caitlin; Mulligan, Peter; Whetstine, Johnathan R.; Cole, Philip A.; Casero, Robert A.; Shi, Yang
        Cell (Cambridge, MA, United States) (2004), 119 (7), 941-953CODEN: CELLB5; ISSN:0092-8674. (Cell Press)
        Posttranslational modifications of histone N-terminal tails impact chromatin structure and gene transcription. While the extent of histone acetylation is detd. by both acetyltransferases and deacetylases, it has been unclear whether histone methylation is also regulated by enzymes with opposing activities. Here, we provide evidence that LSD1 (KIAA0601), a nuclear homolog of amine oxidases, functions as a histone demethylase and transcriptional corepressor. LSD1 specifically demethylates histone H3 lysine 4, which is linked to active transcription. Lysine demethylation occurs via an oxidn. reaction that generates formaldehyde. Importantly, RNAi inhibition of LSD1 causes an increase in H3 lysine 4 methylation and concomitant derepression of target genes, suggesting that LSD1 represses transcription via histone demethylation. The results thus identify a histone demethylase conserved from S. pombe to human and reveal dynamic regulation of histone methylation by both histone methylases and demethylases.

        A Novel Mammalian Flavin-dependent Histone Demethylase
        Karytinos, Aristotele; Forneris, Federico; Profumo, Antonella; Ciossani, Giuseppe; Battaglioli, Elena; Binda, Claudia; Mattevi, Andrea
        Journal of Biological Chemistry (2009), 284 (26), 17775-17782CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
        Methylation of Lys residues on histone proteins is a well known and extensively characterized epigenetic mark. The recent discovery of lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1) demonstrated that lysine methylation can be dynamically controlled. Among the histone demethylases so far identified, LSD1 has the unique feature of functioning through a flavin-dependent amine oxidn. reaction. Data base anal. reveals that mammalian genomes contain a gene (AOF1, for amine-oxidase flavin-contg. domain 1) that is homologous to the LSD1-coding gene. Here, we demonstrate that the protein encoded by AOF1 represents a second mammalian flavin-dependent histone demethylase, named LSD2. The new demethylase is strictly specific for mono- and dimethylated Lys4 of histone H3, recognizes a long stretch of the H3 N-terminal tail, senses the presence of addnl. epigenetic marks on the histone substrate, and is covalently inhibited by tranylcypromine. As opposed to LSD1, LSD2 does not form a biochem. stable complex with the C-terminal domain of the corepressor protein CoREST. Furthermore, LSD2 contains a CW-type zinc finger motif with potential zinc-binding sites that are not present in LSD1. We conclude that mammalian LSD2 represents a new flavin-dependent H3-Lys4 demethylase that features substrate specificity properties highly similar to those of LSD1 but is very likely to be part of chromatin-remodeling complexes that are distinct from those involving LSD1.

        LSD1 inhibition: a therapeutic strategy in cancer?
        Lynch, James T.; Harris, William J.; Somervaille, Tim C. P.

        A review. Introduction: The role of epigenetic dysfunction in cancer is increasingly appreciated. This has raised the question as to whether enzymes that regulate the structure and function of chromatin might represent novel therapeutic targets. The histone demethylase LSD1 is one such candidate and novel, potent inhibitors are under development.Areas covered: The literature on LSD1 (also known as KDM1A, AOF2, BHC110 or KIAA0601) was identified in Pubmed and is herein discussed. Areas covered include the structure and enzymic activity of LSD1, its role in chromatin regulatory complexes, its functional roles in normal and malignant tissue, pharmacol. inhibitors of its activity and their putative therapeutic roles.Expert opinion: Pre-clin. data supporting a therapeutic role for LSD1 inhibitors are most encouraging in acute myeloid leukemia, although optimal dosing strategies and beneficial combinations with other agents remain unclear. Studies making use of potent, selective LSD1 inhibitors active in the nanomolar range are required to establish therapeutic indications in other subtypes of haematol. malignancy, and in solid tumors.

        LSD1 demethylates repressive histone marks to promote androgen-receptor-dependent transcription
        Metzger, Eric; Wissmann, Melanie; Yin, Na; Mueller, Judith M.; Schneider, Robert; Peters, Antoine H. F. M.; Guenther, Thomas; Buettner, Reinhard; Schuele, Roland
        Nature (London, United Kingdom) (2005), 437 (7057), 436-439CODEN: NATUAS; ISSN:0028-0836. (Nature Publishing Group)
        Gene regulation in eukaryotes requires the coordinate interaction of chromatin-modulating proteins with specific transcription factors such as the androgen receptor. Gene activation and repression is specifically regulated by histone methylation status at distinct lysine residues. Here the authors show that lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1; also known as BHC110) co-localizes with the androgen receptor in normal human prostate and prostate tumor. LSD1 interacts with androgen receptor in vitro and in vivo, and stimulates androgen-receptor-dependent transcription. Conversely, knockdown of LSD1 protein levels abrogates androgen-induced transcriptional activation and cell proliferation. Chromatin immunopptn. analyses demonstrate that androgen receptor and LSD1 form chromatin-assocd. complexes in a ligand-dependent manner. LSD1 relieves repressive histone marks by demethylation of histone H3 at lysine 9 (H3-K9), thereby leading to de-repression of androgen receptor target genes. Furthermore, the authors identify pargyline as an inhibitor of LSD1. Pargyline blocks demethylation of H3-K9 by LSD1 and consequently androgen-receptor-dependent transcription. Thus, modulation of LSD1 activity offers a new strategy to regulate androgen receptor functions. Here, the authors link demethylation of a repressive histone mark with androgen-receptor-dependent gene activation, thus providing a mechanism by which demethylases control specific gene expression.

        Breast carcinogenesis is a multistep process involving both genetic and epigenetic changes.         Lysine-specific demethylase 1 is strongly expressed in poorly differentiated neuroblastoma: Implications for therapy
        Schulte, Johannes H.; Lim, Soyoung; Schramm, Alexander; Friedrichs, Nicolaus; Koster, Jan; Versteeg, Rogier; Ora, Ingrid; Pajtler, Kristian; Klein-Hitpass, Ludger; Kuhfittig-Kulle, Steffi; Metzger, Eric; Schuele, Roland; Eggert, Angelika; Buettner, Reinhard; Kirfel, Jutta
        Cancer Research (2009), 69 (5), 2065-2071CODEN: CNREA8; ISSN:0008-5472. (American Association for Cancer Research)
        Aberrant epigenetic changes in DNA methylation and histone acetylation are hallmarks of most cancers, whereas histone methylation was previously considered to be irreversible and less versatile. Recently, several histone demethylases were identified catalyzing the removal of Me groups from histone H3 lysine residues and thereby influencing gene expression. Neuroblastomas continue to remain a clin. challenge despite advances in multimodal therapy. Here, we address the functional significance of the chromatin-modifying enzyme lysine-specific demethylase 1 (LSD1) in neuroblastoma. LSD1 expression correlated with adverse outcome and was inversely correlated with differentiation in neuroblastic tumors. Differentiation of neuroblastoma cells resulted in down-regulation of LSD1. Small interfering RNA-mediated knockdown of LSD1 decreased cellular growth, induced expression of differentiation-assocd. genes, and increased target gene-specific H3K4 methylation. Moreover, LSD1 inhibition using monoamine oxidase inhibitors resulted in an increase of global H3K4 methylation and growth inhibition of neuroblastoma cells in vitro. Finally, targeting LSD1 reduced neuroblastoma xenograft growth in vivo. Here, we provide the first evidence that a histone demethylase, LSD1, is involved in maintaining the undifferentiated, malignant phenotype of neuroblastoma cells. We show that inhibition of LSD1 reprograms the transcriptome of neuroblastoma cells and inhibits neuroblastoma xenograft growth. Our results suggest that targeting histone demethylases may provide a novel option for cancer therapy.

        Histone H3 Lysine 4 Demethylation Is a Target of Nonselective Antidepressive Medications
        Lee, Min Gyu; Wynder, Christopher; Schmidt, Dawn M.; McCafferty, Dewey G.; Shiekhattar, Ramin
        Chemistry & Biology (Cambridge, MA, United States) (2006), 13 (6), 563-567CODEN: CBOLE2; ISSN:1074-5521. (Cell Press)
        Demethylation of histone H3 lysine 4 is carried out by BHC110/LSD1, an enzyme with close homol. to monoamine oxidases (MAO). Monoamine oxidase A or B are frequent targets of selective and nonselective small mol. inhibitors used for treatment of depression. Here the authors show that in contrast to selective monoamine oxidase inhibitors such as pargyline, nonselective monoamine oxidase inhibitors potently inhibit nucleosomal demethylation of histone H3 lysine 4. Treatment of P19 embryonal carcinoma cells with tranylcypromine resulted in global increase in H3K4 methylation as well as transcriptional derepression of two BHC110 target genes, Egr1 and the pluripotent stem cell marker Oct4. These results attest to the effectiveness of tranylcypromine as a small mol. inhibitor of histone demethylation.
